        <title>A draft of the genome and four transcriptomes of a medicinal and pesticidal angiosperm Azadirachta indica | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="The Azadirachta indica (neem) tree is a source of a wide number of natural products, including the potent biopesticide azadirachtin. In spite of its widespread applications in agriculture and medicine, the molecular aspects of the biosynthesis of neem terpenoids remain largely unexplored. The current report describes the draft genome and four transcriptomes of A. indica and attempts to contextualise the sequence information in terms of its molecular phylogeny, transcript expression and terpenoid biosynthesis pathways. A. indica is the first member of the family Meliaceae to be sequenced using next generation sequencing approach. The genome and transcriptomes of A. indica were sequenced using multiple sequencing platforms and libraries. The A. indica genome is AT-rich, bears few repetitive DNA elements and comprises about 20,000 genes. The molecular phylogenetic analyses grouped A. indica together with Citrus sinensis from the Rutaceae family validating its conventional taxonomic classification. Comparative transcript expression analysis showed either exclusive or enhanced expression of known genes involved in neem terpenoid biosynthesis pathways compared to other sequenced angiosperms. Genome and transcriptome analyses in A. indica led to the identification of repeat elements, nucleotide composition and expression profiles of genes in various organs. This study on A. indica genome and transcriptomes will provide a model for characterization of metabolic pathways involved in synthesis of bioactive compounds, comparative evolutionary studies among various Meliaceae family members and help annotate their genomes. A better understanding of molecular pathways involved in the azadirachtin synthesis in A. indica will pave ways for bulk production of environment friendly biopesticides."/>

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

        <script class="js-entry">
    if (window.config.mustardcut) {
        (function(w, d) {
            
            
            
                window.Component = {};
            

            var currentScript = d.currentScript || d.head.querySelector('script.js-entry');

            
            function catchNoModuleSupport() {
                var scriptEl = d.createElement('script');
                return (!('noModule' in scriptEl) && 'onbeforeload' in scriptEl)
            }

            var headScripts = [
                {'src': '/static/js/polyfill-es5-bundle-974eb189f7.js', 'async': false},
                {'src': '/static/js/airbrake-es5-bundle-07006365c1.js', 'async': false},
            ];

            var bodyScripts = [
                {'src': '/static/js/app-es5-bundle-b7e9f20f59.js', 'async': false, 'module': false},
                {'src': '/static/js/app-es6-bundle-0a469a101d.js', 'async': false, 'module': true}
                
                
                    , {'src': '/static/js/global-article-es5-bundle-8c8fdb8bd3.js', 'async': false, 'module': false},
                    {'src': '/static/js/global-article-es6-bundle-e6af530740.js', 'async': false, 'module': true}
                
            ];

            function createScript(script) {
                var scriptEl = d.createElement('script');
                scriptEl.src = script.src;
                scriptEl.async = script.async;
                if (script.module === true) {
                    scriptEl.type = "module";
                    if (catchNoModuleSupport()) {
                        scriptEl.src = '';
                    }
                } else if (script.module === false) {
                    scriptEl.setAttribute('nomodule', true)
                }
                if (script.charset) {
                    scriptEl.setAttribute('charset', script.charset);
                }

                return scriptEl;
            }

            for (var i = 0; i < headScripts.length; ++i) {
                var scriptEl = createScript(headScripts[i]);
                currentScript.parentNode.insertBefore(scriptEl, currentScript.nextSibling);
            }

            d.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function() {
                for (var i = 0; i < bodyScripts.length; ++i) {
                    var scriptEl = createScript(bodyScripts[i]);
                    d.body.appendChild(scriptEl);
                }
            });

            // Webfont repeat view
            var config = w.config;
            if (config && config.publisherBrand && sessionStorage.fontsLoaded === 'true') {
                d.documentElement.className += ' webfonts-loaded';
            }
        })(window, document);

        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="A draft of the genome and four transcriptomes of a medicinal and pesticidal angiosperm Azadirachta indica"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="The Azadirachta indica (neem) tree is a source of a wide number of natural products, including the potent biopesticide azadirachtin. In spite of its widespread applications in agriculture and medicine, the molecular aspects of the biosynthesis of neem terpenoids remain largely unexplored. The current report describes the draft genome and four transcriptomes of A. indica and attempts to contextualise the sequence information in terms of its molecular phylogeny, transcript expression and terpenoid biosynthesis pathways. A. indica is the first member of the family Meliaceae to be sequenced using next generation sequencing approach. The genome and transcriptomes of A. indica were sequenced using multiple sequencing platforms and libraries. The A. indica genome is AT-rich, bears few repetitive DNA elements and comprises about 20,000 genes. The molecular phylogenetic analyses grouped A. indica together with Citrus sinensis from the Rutaceae family validating its conventional taxonomic classification. Comparative transcript expression analysis showed either exclusive or enhanced expression of known genes involved in neem terpenoid biosynthesis pathways compared to other sequenced angiosperms. Genome and transcriptome analyses in A. indica led to the identification of repeat elements, nucleotide composition and expression profiles of genes in various organs. This study on A. indica genome and transcriptomes will provide a model for characterization of metabolic pathways involved in synthesis of bioactive compounds, comparative evolutionary studies among various Meliaceae family members and help annotate their genomes. A better understanding of molecular pathways involved in the azadirachtin synthesis in A. indica will pave ways for bulk production of environment friendly biopesticides."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>The <i>Azadirachta indica</i> (neem) tree is a source of a wide number of natural products, including the potent biopesticide azadirachtin. In spite of its widespread applications in agriculture and medicine, the molecular aspects of the biosynthesis of neem terpenoids remain largely unexplored. The current report describes the draft genome and four transcriptomes of <i>A. indica</i> and attempts to contextualise the sequence information in terms of its molecular phylogeny, transcript expression and terpenoid biosynthesis pathways. <i>A. indica</i> is the first member of the family <i>Meliaceae</i> to be sequenced using next generation sequencing approach.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>The genome and transcriptomes of <i>A. indica</i> were sequenced using multiple sequencing platforms and libraries. The <i>A. indica</i> genome is AT-rich, bears few repetitive DNA elements and comprises about 20,000 genes. The molecular phylogenetic analyses grouped <i>A. indica</i> together with <i>Citrus sinensis</i> from the <i>Rutaceae</i> family validating its conventional taxonomic classification. Comparative transcript expression analysis showed either exclusive or enhanced expression of known genes involved in neem terpenoid biosynthesis pathways compared to other sequenced angiosperms. Genome and transcriptome analyses in <i>A. indica</i> led to the identification of repeat elements, nucleotide composition and expression profiles of genes in various organs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>This study on <i>A. indica</i> genome and transcriptomes will provide a model for characterization of metabolic pathways involved in synthesis of bioactive compounds, comparative evolutionary studies among various <i>Meliaceae</i> family members and help annotate their genomes. A better understanding of molecular pathways involved in the azadirachtin synthesis in <i>A. indica</i> will pave ways for bulk production of environment friendly biopesticides.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Azadirachta indica</i> A. Juss (neem) is an evergreen tree native to the Indian subcontinent[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schmutterer H: The neem tree : Azadirachta indica A. Juss. and other meliaceous plants : sources of unique natural products for integrated pest management, medicine, industry, and other purposes. Neem Foundation. 2002" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d54360e660">1</a>] belonging to the family <i>Meliaceae</i> and order <i>Rutales</i>. The neem tree is extensively studied for its natural products. Neem oil and its limonoids such as azadirachtin, nimbin, salannin among others are of substantial economic value due to their wide array of applications in agriculture[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Brahmachari G: Neem–an omnipotent plant: a retrospection. Chembiochem : a European journal of chemical biology. 2004, 5 (4): 408-421. 10.1002/cbic.200300749." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d54360e669">2</a>], healthcare[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Osujih M: Exploration of the frontiers of tradomedical practices: basis for development of alternative medical healthcare services in developing countries. J R Soc Health. 1993, 113 (4): 190-194. 10.1177/146642409311300409." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54360e672">3</a>] and soil conservation[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Saxena RC: In Neem: Today and in the New Millenium. 2004, Dordrecht: Kluwer Academic Publishers" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54360e676">4</a>]. Azadirachtin, isolated half a century ago, has been intensively studied and its commercial formulations have been found to be toxic against a large range of insect species, whilst retaining very low mammalian toxicity[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Ley SV: Synthesis and chemistry of the insect antifeedant azadirachtin. Pure &amp; Appl Chem. 1994, 66 (10-11): 4-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54360e679">5</a>]. Recent success in the total synthesis of azadirachtin[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Veitch GE, Boyer A, Ley SV: The azadirachtin story. Angew Chem Int Ed Engl. 2008, 47 (49): 9402-9429. 10.1002/anie.200802675." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54360e682">6</a>] has renewed interest towards its commercial exploitation. Neem-derived azadirachtin and other limonoids are also used as anti-proliferative[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schumacher M, Cerella C, Reuter S, Dicato M, Diederich M: Anti-inflammatory, pro-apoptotic, and anti-proliferative effects of a methanolic neem (Azadirachta indica) leaf extract are mediated via modulation of the nuclear factor-kappaB pathway. Genes &amp; nutrition. 2011, 6 (2): 149-160. 10.1007/s12263-010-0194-6." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54360e685">7</a>], cytotoxic[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Harish Kumar G, Chandra Mohan KV, Jagannadha Rao A, Nagini S: Nimbolide a limonoid from Azadirachta indica inhibits proliferation and induces apoptosis of human choriocarcinoma (BeWo) cells. Investigational new drugs. 2009, 27 (3): 246-252. 10.1007/s10637-008-9170-z." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d54360e688">8</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Roy MK, Kobori M, Takenaka M, Nakahara K, Shinmoto H, Isobe S, Tsushida T: Antiproliferative effect on human cancer cell lines after treatment with nimbolide extracted from an edible part of the neem tree (Azadirachta indica). Phytotherapy research : PTR. 2007, 21 (3): 245-250. 10.1002/ptr.2058." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d54360e691">10</a>], larvicidal[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Okumu FO, Knols BG, Fillinger U: Larvicidal effects of a neem (Azadirachta indica) oil formulation on the malaria vector Anopheles gambiae. Malar J. 2007, 6: 63-10.1186/1475-2875-6-63." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d54360e695">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Denardi SE, Bechara GH, de Oliveira PR, Camargo Mathias MI: Inhibitory action of neem aqueous extract (Azadirachta indica A. Juss) on the vitellogenesis of Rhipicephalus sanguineus (Latreille, 1806) (Acari: Ixodidae) ticks. Microsc Res Tech. 2011, 74 (10): 889-899. 10.1002/jemt.20973." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d54360e698">12</a>], and anti-inflammatory[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Schumacher M, Cerella C, Reuter S, Dicato M, Diederich M: Anti-inflammatory, pro-apoptotic, and anti-proliferative effects of a methanolic neem (Azadirachta indica) leaf extract are mediated via modulation of the nuclear factor-kappaB pathway. Genes &amp; nutrition. 2011, 6 (2): 149-160. 10.1007/s12263-010-0194-6." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54360e701">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Thoh M, Kumar P, Nagarajaram HA, Manna SK: Azadirachtin interacts with the tumor necrosis factor (TNF) binding domain of its receptors and inhibits TNF-induced biological responses. J Biol Chem. 2010, 285 (8): 5888-5895. 10.1074/jbc.M109.065847." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d54360e704">13</a>] agents, suggesting the need for better understanding of molecular pathways involved in their synthesis.</p><p>In spite of the varied uses of azadirachtin and other neem-derived limonoids, a modern agro-chemical and/or pharmaceutical program focusing on understanding their molecular mechanism(s) of action is yet to be established. A better understanding of the biology of differentiation of secretory cells known to harbor azadirachtin and other triterpenoids[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Johnson S, Morgan ED, Peiris CN: Development of the major triterpenoids and oil in the fruit and seeds of neem (Azadirachta indica). Ann Bot. 1996, 78: 6-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d54360e710">14</a>] may permit development of varieties with higher percentage of these cells in the cotyledons. This information may also be exploited in stalling or delaying further differentiation of these cells to permit greater accumulation of the terpenoids of interest. The elucidation of complete pathways leading to terpenoid biosynthesis and expression of genes involved in such pathways in <i>A. indica</i> will pave ways towards development of newer terpenoid-based biotechnological applications.</p><p><i>De novo</i> sequencing and assembly of the transcriptome from <i>A. indica</i> fruit was reported previously[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Krishnan NM, Pattnaik S, Deepak SA, Hariharan AH, Gaur P, Chaudhary R, Jain P, Vaidyanathan S, Krishna B, Panda B: De novo sequencing and assembly of Azadirachta indica fruit transcriptome. Curr Sci (India). 2011, 101 (12): 1553-1561." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54360e724">15</a>]. This report extends the earlier study to cover the whole genome and transcriptomes from root, leaf, stem and flower of neem. Comparative genomics among the <i>Meliaceae</i> family members will be enabled and, cheaper and environment friendly biopesticides may result using results presented in this study.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and similarity with other plant genomes</h3><p>Sequencing libraries generated using multiple platforms and chemistries were tested to assemble the neem genome and transcriptomes (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Tab1">1</a>, Table S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>). Various combinations of sequencing libraries were used to obtain the best scaffold N50s and N90s using SOAPdenovo[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li R, Zhu H, Ruan J, Qian W, Fang X, Shi Z, Li Y, Li S, Shan G, Kristiansen K, et al: De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010, 20 (2): 265-272. 10.1101/gr.097261.109." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d54360e748">16</a>]. The scheme for <i>A. indica</i> genome assembly is given in Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig1">1</a>a. The <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>A. indica</i> genome produced a scaffold N50 length of 452,028 bp with corresponding scaffold N90 length of 56,222 bp, contig N50 length of 740 bp and contig N90 length of 172 bp. The genome size of neem was determined to be 364 Mbp using the kmer frequency plot (Figure S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>) representing 95% of the total published genome size[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ohri D, Bhargava A, Chatterjee A: Nuclear DNA amounts in 112 species of tropical hardwoods – new estimates. Plant biology. 2004, 6 (5): 555-561. 10.1055/s-2004-821235." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54360e767">17</a>]. Chargaff's symmetry rule was applied for assessing the quality of the genome assembly[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yamagishi MEB, Hirai RH: Chargaff’s “Grammar of Biology”: New Fractal-like Rules. 2011, arXiv" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54360e770">18</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Prabhu VV: Symmetry observations in long nucleotide sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 1993, 21 (12): 2797-2800. 10.1093/nar/21.12.2797." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d54360e773">21</a>] by comparing the symmetry of 4-mers between the <i>A. indica</i> scaffolds and the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana (A. thaliana)</i> genome. There was a tighter distribution of symmetry in <i>A. indica</i> at around 0.5 (Figure S2 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>) compared to the <i>A. thaliana</i> suggesting good assembly results. Genome scaffolds (&gt;N50) mapping with <i>Citrus sinensis</i> (<i>C. sinensis)</i> and <i>Citrus clementina</i> (<i>C. clementina)</i> showed conserved macrosynteny between neem and citrus (Figure S3 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>) but not with chromosomes from <i>A thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> (<i>O sativa</i>), <i>Theobroma cacao</i> (<i>T cacao)</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (<i>V vinifera), Ricinus communis</i> (<i>R communis</i>) and <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (<i>S. bicolor</i>) (Figure S3 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). In <i>A. indica</i>, 385 and 435 scaffolds (&gt;N50) out of a total of 534 scaffolds (&gt;N50) mapped to <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>C. clementina</i> genome scaffolds (&gt;N50), respectively, with an Expect value of zero. Phylogenetic bootstrap analysis[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Felsenstein J: PHYLIP - Phylogeny Inference Package (Version 3.2). Cladistics. 1989, 5: 164-166." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54360e853">22</a>] corroborated the taxonomic closeness of <i>A. indica</i> to <i>C. sinensis</i>, as indicated by 100% bootstrap value using <i>rbcL</i> and <i>rbcS</i> (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig2">2</a>a). Phylogenetic analysis within the <i>Meliaceae</i> family grouped <i>A. indica</i> with <i>Owenia</i> and <i>Melia</i> sp. with a 100% bootstrap value (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig2">2</a>b). Like <i>A. indica</i>, <i>Melia azadirach</i> is known to bear bioactive limonoids[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tan QG, Luo XD: Meliaceous limonoids: chemistry and biological activities. Chem Rev. 2011, 111 (11): 7437-7522. 10.1021/cr9004023." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d54360e894">23</a>] suggesting a common evolutionary trajectory with regard to synthesis of these compounds.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 
                          <b><i>de novo</i></b>
                          <b>assembly statistics</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>

                          <b><i>A. indica</i></b>

                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>Molecular phylogeny of</b> <b><i>A. indica</i></b> <b>with plants of other family</b> <b>(a) and within the</b> <b><i>Meliaceae</i></b> <b>family (b).</b> Individual rbcL and rbcS alignments (*.phy) were concatenated and bootstrapped using 100 replicates (<b>a</b>) through 'seqboot' in PHYLIP version 3.69[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Felsenstein J: PHYLIP - Phylogeny Inference Package (Version 3.2). Cladistics. 1989, 5: 164-166." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1421">22</a>]. For intra-family phylogeny, rbcL sequences were bootstrapped (<b>b</b>). Phylogenies for each replicate was reconstructed using the 'dnaml' option in Phylip, after choosing either <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> (<b>a</b>) or <i>Citrus sinensis</i> (<b>b</b>) as outgroups. The consensus phylogeny with bootstrap values highlighting the branch support was plotted using 'consense' and 'drawgram' options in Phylip.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat analyses</h3><p>Repeat Modeler (<i>RepeatModeler Open-1.0</i>, 2008–2010)[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Repeat Modeller.&#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1459">24</a>] and Repeat Masker (<i>RepeatMasker Open-3.0</i>, 1996–2010)[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Repeat Masker.&#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1465">25</a>] were used to determine the <i>de novo</i> repeats and to perform homology-based repeat analysis respectively among <i>A. indica</i> genome repeats. For repeat analysis, we used consensus repeat libraries (of <i>R.communis</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chan AP, Crabtree J, Zhao Q, Lorenzi H, Orvis J, Puiu D, Melake-Berhan A, Jones KM, Redman J, Chen G, et al, et al: Draft genome sequence of the oilseed species Ricinus communis. Nat Biotechnol. 2010, 28 (9): 951-956. 10.1038/nbt.1674." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1478">26</a>], <i>Glycine max (G.max)</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Schmutz J, Cannon SB, Schlueter J, Ma J, Mitros T, Nelson W, Hyten DL, Song Q, Thelen JJ, Cheng J, et al: Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature. 2010, 463 (7278): 178-183. 10.1038/nature08670." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1484">27</a>] and <i>S. bicolor</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Paterson AH, Bowers JE, Bruggmann R, Dubchak I, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hellsten U, Mitros T, Poliakov A, et al, et al: The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 2009, 457 (7229): 551-556. 10.1038/nature07723." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1491">28</a>]), in-built plant specific libraries, Repbase[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jurka J: Repbase update: a database and an electronic journal of repetitive elements. Trends in genetics : TIG. 2000, 16 (9): 418-420. 10.1016/S0168-9525(00)02093-X." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1494">29</a>] and the <i>de novo</i> neem repeat library constructed using Repeat Modeler. In addition, LTR-finder[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu Z, Wang H: LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35: W265-268. 10.1093/nar/gkm286. Web Server issue" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1500">30</a>], TransposonPSI[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="TransposonPSI.&#xA;                    http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1503">31</a>] and MITE-hunter[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Han Y, Wessler SR: MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 2010, 38 (22): e199-10.1093/nar/gkq862." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1506">32</a>] were used to study repeats in the <i>A. indica</i> genome. The neem genome was found to harbour fewer repeat elements compared to other sequenced eudicots with a total non-redundant repeat content of 13.03%. We further classified the repeats into two categories, the interspersed elements (11.21%) comprising of retrotransposons (3.72%) and DNA transposons (0.75%), and the tandem repeats (3.58%) comprising of the low complexity repeats (3.17%), simple repeats (0.05%), and the satellite repeats (0.38%) (Table S2 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>). The detected interspersed repeat sequences identified using different tools (with similarity of at least 80% and more than 200 nucleotides long) were clustered using Vmatch[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Vmatch.&#xA;                    http://www.vmatch.de&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1516">33</a>], giving rise to a consensus neem repeat element library of length 23,920,944 bp (~6.59% of the genome).</p><p>We studied the insertion age distributions among the LTR-retrotransposons in <i>A. indica</i> made by the Kimura 2-parameter distance matrix[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kimura M: A simple method for estimating evolutionary rates of base substitutions through comparative studies of nucleotide sequences. J Mol Evol. 1980, 16 (2): 111-120. 10.1007/BF01731581." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1525">34</a>] and inferred that the oldest insertion event in neem dated back to 13.35 million years (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig3">3</a>). The frequency of insertion events peaks around 1 million years ago with smaller secondary peaks observed at ~6 million and ~11 million years ago respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>
                          <b>The frequency distribution of LTR-retrotransposon insertion ages in</b>
                          <b><i>A. indica</i></b>
                          <b>.</b>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5"><i>De novo</i> transcriptome assembly</h3><p>The scheme for transcriptome assembly and annotation is provided in Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig1">1</a>b. The transcriptomes of different organs were independently assembled using Inchworm (a part of the <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembler Trinity[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, et al: Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 29 (7): 644-652. 10.1038/nbt.1883." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1575">35</a>]). Trinity performs better in comparison to other <i>de novo</i> transcriptome assembly programs for a wide range of parameters like genome complexity, read coverage and spliced isoforms[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, et al: Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 29 (7): 644-652. 10.1038/nbt.1883." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1581">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhao QY, Wang Y, Kong YM, Luo D, Li X, Hao P: Optimizing de novo transcriptome assembly from short-read RNA-Seq data: a comparative study. BMC Bioinforma. 2011, 12 Suppl 14: S2-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1585">36</a>] and hence was a natural choice for <i>A. indica</i> transcriptome assembly. Chimeric transcripts observed during Inchworm assembly[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="van Bakel H, Stout JM, Cote AG, Tallon CM, Sharpe AG, Hughes TR, Page JE: The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 2011, 12 (10): R102-10.1186/gb-2011-12-10-r102." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1591">37</a>] were filtered using PASA (Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments)[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Smith RK, Hannick LI, Maiti R, Ronning CM, Rusch DB, Town CD, et al, et al: Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (19): 5654-5666. 10.1093/nar/gkg770." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1594">38</a>]. The PASA-filtered transcripts were then anchored to the genome scaffolds by assembling overlapped transcript alignments into maximal alignment assemblies in order to remove multiple spliced isoforms.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Transcript annotation and gene prediction analysis</h3><p>The transcriptome assemblies were annotated using the KEGG Automatic Annotation Server (KAAS)[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Moriya Y, Itoh M, Okuda S, Yoshizawa AC, Kanehisa M: KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35: W182-185. 10.1093/nar/gkm321. Web Server issue)" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1605">39</a>]. In addition to the KAAS, the assemblies were serially annotated using; MegaBLAST[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215 (3): 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1608">40</a>] against the non-redundant nucleotide database, BlastX against the non-redundant protein database, MegaBlast against the RefSeqRNA, Expressed Sequence Tag (EST), Transcriptome Shotgun Assembly (TSA) databases[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215 (3): 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1611">40</a>] and AutoFACT[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Koski LB, Gray MW, Lang BF, Burger G: AutoFACT: an automatic functional annotation and classification tool. BMC Bioinforma. 2005, 6: 151-10.1186/1471-2105-6-151." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1614">41</a>] using uniref90 and uniref100[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Suzek BE, Huang H, McGarvey P, Mazumder R, Wu CH: UniRef: comprehensive and non-redundant UniProt reference clusters. Bioinformatics. 2007, 23 (10): 1282-1288. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btm098." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1617">42</a>], kegg[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S: KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000, 28 (1): 27-30. 10.1093/nar/28.1.27." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1621">43</a>], cog[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Tatusov RL, Galperin MY, Natale DA, Koonin EV: The COG database: a tool for genome-scale analysis of protein functions and evolution. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000, 28 (1): 33-36. 10.1093/nar/28.1.33." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1624">44</a>] and nr[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215 (3): 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1627">40</a>] databases (Table S3 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>). In total, 380,629 common and 1,374 unique KEGG annotations (KO terms) across all organs (Figure S4 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>) were observed. Annotated/predicted genes in <i>A. indica</i> were compared with a non-redundant database containing green plants gene identifier (gi) lists using homology-based searches. As shown in Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig4">4</a>, a higher percentage of overlap was found between <i>A. indica</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>, a plant known to express a host of terpernoids[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jaillon O, Aury JM, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A, Choisne N, Aubourg S, Vitulo N, Jubin C, et al: The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature. 2007, 449 (7161): 463-467. 10.1038/nature06148." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1649">45</a>]. As expected, genes common between <i>A. indica</i> and other species compared (<i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and in four sequenced organs of <i>A. indica</i>) had the highest number of KEGG annotation associated with them (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig4">4</a>). The transcripts from all organs were further assigned Gene Ontology (GO) terms using Blast2GO[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Conesa A, Gotz S, Garcia-Gomez JM, Terol J, Talon M, Robles M: Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics. 2005, 21 (18): 3674-3676. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bti610." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1671">46</a>] (Figure S5 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a> and Table S4 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>Gene annotations and comparison across species.</b> Predicted genes in <i>A. indica</i> were used for homology-based search against the nr database restricting the hits to the green plant gene identifiers. Similar analyses were performed using the annotations in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>O. sativa.</i> The overlapping gi list were used to draw the Venn diagram.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>A. indica</i> RNA-Seq reads were aligned to the genomes or genome scaffolds of multiple plant species. The alignments of <i>A. indica</i> RNA-Seq reads to <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>C. clementina</i> genome scaffolds by TopHat[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Trapnell C, Pachter L, Salzberg SL: TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics. 2009, 25 (9): 1105-1111. 10.1093/bioinformatics/btp120." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1728">47</a>] and CuffLinks[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Trapnell C, Williams BA, Pertea G, Mortazavi A, Kwan G, van Baren MJ, Salzberg SL, Wold BJ, Pachter L: Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat Biotechnol. 2010, 28 (5): 511-515. 10.1038/nbt.1621." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1732">48</a>] suggest sequence similarity between neem and citrus (Table S5 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>). This is in agreement with the results obtained from our phylogenetic analysis (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig2">2</a>a). We built training sets using <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>C. clementina</i> gene models and used GlimmerHMM[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL: TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004, 20 (16): 2878-2879. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bth315." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1747">49</a>] to subsequently predict genes in <i>A. indica</i>. Additionally, we used the <i>A. thaliana</i> training sets to represent a distantly related species for gene prediction analysis<i>.</i></p><p>GlimmerHMM[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL: TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004, 20 (16): 2878-2879. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bth315." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1762">49</a>] predicted greater number of gene models in <i>A. indica</i> using <i>C. sinensis</i> (34,624) and <i>C. clementina</i> (34,737) training sets compared to <i>A.thaliana</i> (23,397). The predicted genes were then serially annotated using Megablast[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215 (3): 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1778">40</a>] and TblastX[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ: Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990, 215 (3): 403-410." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1781">40</a>] (with Expect value of 10<sup>-10</sup>) resulting in 22,760 and 22,840 annotations with <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>C. clementina</i> respectively. Statistics for PASA[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Smith RK, Hannick LI, Maiti R, Ronning CM, Rusch DB, Town CD, et al, et al: Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (19): 5654-5666. 10.1093/nar/gkg770." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1792">38</a>] predicted mapped transcripts, gene structures and assembly is provided in Table S6 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>. The overlap between the GlimmerHMM and PASA predicted gene models was marginally higher using the <i>C. clementina</i> training set compared to the <i>C. sinensis</i> training set (Figure S6 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). The genes predicted by GlimmerHMM using <i>C. sinensis</i> training set and training set candidates predicted by PASA identified 10,667, 10,965, 12,209 and 11,181 genes expressed in the root, leaf, stem and flower, respectively (Figure S6c in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Nucleotide composition, transcript expression and gene structure analysis</h3><p>The nucleotide composition analyses in the transcriptome from all organs suggested different Adenine (A), Thymine (T), and Guanine (G) Cytosine (C) content distribution. The A + T content of most transcripts ranged between 20-40% while for G + C there were two distinct populations of transcripts, one with 0-20% and the other with 20-40% (Figure S7 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><p>Fragments per transcript kilobase per million fragment (FPKM) values derived from Trinity transcriptome assembly pipeline were used for expression analysis in different organs of neem (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig1">1</a>c). The FPKM values for transcripts with overlapping peaks at the same level of expression in all organs were plotted (Figure S8 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). The frequency histograms plotted after binning the transcripts suggested maximum density around ~40% GC with 5.0-5.5 log<sub>2</sub> (FPKM) values (Figure S9 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). In order to avoid erroneous expression analysis resulting from genes with multiple isoforms, the PASA-assembly pipeline were used to remove them and infer relative abundance of transcripts in different organs (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>Organ-specific expression for un-annotated transcripts.</b> KEGG annotations obtained for transcripts from all organs were parsed to retain only the un-annotated ones. PASA assemblies, establishing correspondence between transcripts and common genomic loci were obtained for the same. The top 300 highly expressed transcripts for each organ were chosen, filtered for redundancies, to yield a total of 888 unique assemblies and plotted.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Post-annotation, GC content comparisons were made between the genome versus transcriptome, introns versus exons and first introns versus first exons (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig6">6</a>). The raw genomic reads were relatively GC-poor and the exons were relatively GC-rich (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig6">6</a>). The genes those were inferred to accumulate to higher abundances in neem harbored longer introns when compared to the ones in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig7">7</a>). Although, there was variation in the exon size, similar correlation between the cumulative exon length and expression was observed.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>GC density profiles.</b> The %GC compositions are plotted as density curves, (<b>a</b>). for the raw sequencing reads from the neem genome and pooled transcriptome from all organs and (<b>b</b>) for all exons, all introns, first exon and first intron from the gene structures assembled using PASA, corresponding to the pooled organ transcriptome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Figure 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click"
