aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>Correlation between expression and gene structure.</b> (<b>a</b>) The average intron and exon sizes of the ten most and least expressed genes bearing KEGG Orthology assignments, from <i>A. indica</i>, <i>A.thaliana, O.sativa</i> and <i>V.vinifera</i> are plotted separately for the low and high expression level categories. The error bars indicate standard deviation in the intron and exon sizes respectively. These sizes were obtained from gene structures provided by PASA assemblies mapping these genes to corresponding genome scaffolds. (<b>b</b>) The gene structures depicting the organization and sizes of exons (boxes) and introns (lines) are represented using the web tool StrDraw, for the above mentioned low (left panel) and high (right panel) transcript expression categories in all species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Comparative analysis of transcript expression</h3><p>In neem, transcript abundances (TAs) were compared with their orthologs from other species after inter-species normalization of the expression values with elongation factor 1-alpha (EF1A; NCBI gene ID: 836161). Among the 88 genes that were differentially abundant in neem, transcripts from three genes (GGPS, malZ and polygalacturonase) were expressed at a higher level in <i>A. indica</i> compared to the other three species studied (Table S7 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>). GGPS (geranylgeranyl diphosphate synthase type II, NCBI gene ID: 816377) is involved in terpenoid backbone synthesis, malZ (alpha-glucosidase, NCBI gene ID: 823737) in galactose metabolism and polygalacturonase (NCBI gene ID: 820815) in starch and sucrose metabolism. Unlike GGPS, no other consensus gene was over-expressed to the same magnitude (&lt;10-fold) in neem when compared to the other three species studied. A complete list of the genes and their relative TAs in species is provided in Table S7 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Terpenoid biosynthesis pathways</h3><p>The genes related to quinone, terpenoid and terpenoid-backbone synthesis pathways were identified by KEGG’s KAAS automatic pathway annotation pipeline[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Moriya Y, Itoh M, Okuda S, Yoshizawa AC, Kanehisa M: KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35: W182-185. 10.1093/nar/gkm321. Web Server issue)" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54360e1972">39</a>]. <i>GGPS</i> (NCBI gene ID: 816377), <i>COQ6</i> (NCBI gene ID: 822006) and <i>CLA1</i> (NCBI gene ID: 827230) were among the top 5 differentially expressed genes in neem leaf compared to the other organs (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig8">8</a>). Eight genes (<i>TPS21</i>, NCBI gene ID: 832461; <i>lytB/ispH</i>, NCBI gene ID: 829585; <i>ispE</i>, NCBI gene ID: 817234; <i>GGPS</i>, NCBI gene ID: 816377; +neomenthol dehydrogenase, NCBI gene ID: 825294; <i>FDPS</i>, NCBI gene ID: 827430; <i>FDFT1</i>, NCBI gene ID: 829616 and <i>SQLE</i>, NCBI gene ID: 816814) involved in the synthesis of sesquiterpenes and triterpenes leading to azadirachtin-A were over-expressed in neem compared to <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa, C. sinensis</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>, (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig8">8</a>c). The length of introns in the terpenoid and steroid biosynthesis gene families in <i>A. indica</i> were greater compared to the ones in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa, C. sinensis</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> (Figure S10 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Figure 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-464/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4404_Fig8_HTML.jpg" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p><b>Genes involved in the terpenoid biosynthesis pathways.</b> Organ-wide transcript abundance heat-maps were drawn for top (<b>a</b>) and bottom (<b>b</b>) five transcripts in each organ. The clustering and heat-map analyses were done using R. Differential transcript abundance were calculated between terpenoid pathway related genes expressed in <i>A. indica</i> leaf to those in leaves of <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. sinensis</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>, after normalizing the transcript abundance of all genes to that of elongation factor 1-alpha (EF1A). The differential indices were further log<sub>2</sub>-transformed and represented as cumulative bar histograms for all species (<b>c</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The current study describes the draft genome and transcriptomes from root, stem, leaf and flower<i>.</i> Both neem and citrus belong to the Order <i>Rutales</i> and our phylogenetic studies reaffirmed their taxonomic closeness. Additionally, phylogenetic studies grouped <i>A. indica</i> with the <i>Melia</i> species, one that is also known to harbour bioactive compounds (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig2">2</a>b) suggesting a common evolutionary process with regard to synthesis of these compounds in <i>Meliaceae.</i> Repeat analysis showed low repeat content in <i>A. indica</i> genome compared with other sequenced angiosperms. This could have been due to the presence of xenobiotic terpenoids specific to the plant, which might have been a major impediment for horizontal gene transfer[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Richardson AO, Palmer JD: Horizontal gene transfer in plants. J Exp Bot. 2007, 58 (1): 1-9." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2116">50</a>]. This impediment in horizontal gene transfer could have skewed the conventional host-pathogen interactions countering the accumulation of repeats in <i>A. indica</i> compared to other contemporary angiosperms. Insertion times of LTR elements in neem suggest a major wave of retro-transposition about 1million years ago (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig3">3</a>), similar to the observation made in <i>S. bicolor</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Paterson AH, Bowers JE, Bruggmann R, Dubchak I, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hellsten U, Mitros T, Poliakov A, et al, et al: The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 2009, 457 (7229): 551-556. 10.1038/nature07723." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2129">28</a>]. However, in <i>A. indica</i> the oldest insertion event date back much further than <i>S. bicolor</i> (13.35 million years in neem against 3 million years in <i>Sorghum</i>, Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig3">3</a>). Monocot genomes have higher GC content than dicots, which is reflected in an average difference in codon usage between them[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Fennoy SL, Bailey-Serres J: Synonymous codon usage in Zea mays L. nuclear genes is varied by levels of C and G-ending codons. Nucleic Acids Res. 1993, 21 (23): 5294-5300. 10.1093/nar/21.23.5294." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2144">51</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Carels N, Bernardi G: Two classes of genes in plants. Genetics. 2000, 154 (4): 1819-1825." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2148">52</a>]. Interestingly, like <i>O. sativa</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wang HC, Hickey DA: Rapid divergence of codon usage patterns within the rice genome. BMC Evol Biol. 2007, S6-7 Suppl 1" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2154">53</a>] but unlike <i>A. thaliana</i> and others[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Banerjee T, Gupta SK, Ghosh TC: Compositional transitions between Oryza sativa and Arabidopsis thaliana genes are linked to the functional change of encoded proteins. Plant Sci. 2000, 170 (2): 7-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2160">54</a>], the <i>A. indica</i> genome shows a bimodal distribution of GC content (Figure S11 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). Domesticated crop genomes with higher GC content are likely to have evolved by a relaxation of a natural selection process against higher nitrogen use in DNA due to the use of nitrogen-rich fertilizers[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Acquisti C, Elser JJ, Kumar S: Ecological nitrogen limitation shapes the DNA composition of plant genomes. Mol Biol Evol. 2009, 26 (5): 953-956. 10.1093/molbev/msp038." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2170">55</a>]. <i>A. indica</i>, a hardy plant, is not domesticated and can grow in poor and degraded soil including in semi-arid conditions with tolerance for high temperature[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Morgan ED: Azadirachtin, a scientific gold mine. Bioorg Med Chem. 2009, 17 (12): 4096-4105. 10.1016/j.bmc.2008.11.081." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2176">56</a>]. Additionally, there is no history of any usage of nitrogen rich fertilizers to grow <i>A. indica</i>. This might have played a role in shaping the AT-rich nucleotide composition of <i>A. indica</i> genome.</p><p>Transcript abundances of genes in <i>A. indica</i> correlated well with their functions in respective organs; for example, root, leaf, stem and flower showed the presence of highly expressed ones involved in ion homeostasis, photosynthesis, ion transport and ATPase activity, respectively. Unlike the genome, <i>A. indica</i> transcriptomes exhibited unimodal GC distribution roughly overlapping with the higher GC peak of the genome (Figure S11 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). The distribution of A + T in <i>A. indica</i> transcriptomes mapped closer to the <i>A. thaliana</i> transcriptome than to the ones in <i>O. sativa</i> or <i>V. vinifera</i> (Figure S11 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM2">2</a>). The lengths and the GC content of the first exons and first introns are reported to be associated with functional elements in other large genomes[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Kalari KR, Casavant M, Bair TB, Keen HL, Comeron JM, Casavant TL, Scheetz TE: First exons and introns–a survey of GC content and gene structure in the human genome. In silico biology. 2006, 6 (3): 237-242." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2213">57</a>]. The GC content of the first exons and first introns in <i>A. indica</i> were similar to that of other exons and introns in the genome (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#Fig7">7</a>). Highly expressed primary transcripts of <i>A. indica</i> bear longer intron structures suggesting their possible functional role in expression and conforming with the results shown in other plants[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Ren XY, Vorst O, Fiers MW, Stiekema WJ, Nap JP: In plants, highly expressed genes are the least compact. Trends in genetics : TIG. 2006, 22 (10): 528-532. 10.1016/j.tig.2006.08.008." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2226">58</a>] and yeast[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Vinogradov AE: Intron length and codon usage. J Mol Evol. 2001, 52 (1): 2-5." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2229">59</a>].</p><p>Sequencing and analysis of the genome and transcriptomes of neem holds significance due to the plant’s utility in agriculture and medicine. The use of botanical products as pest control and deterrence has acquired greater significance with the shifting trends in mainstream agriculture towards sustainable and organic farming. Neem provides a suitable option for developing such eco-friendly and sustainable pesticides[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Morgan ED: Azadirachtin, a scientific gold mine. Bioorg Med Chem. 2009, 17 (12): 4096-4105. 10.1016/j.bmc.2008.11.081." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2235">56</a>]. Currently a very small percentage of farmers use neem-based products as a substitute to synthetic pesticides as the general awareness of such practices remains limited. Azadirachtin, derived from neem seed kernels, has been proved to be a potent and effective pesticide. Total chemical synthesis of azadirachtin is challenging owing to the low yield of synthesis and remains a bottleneck for commercial bulk manufacturing. Secondary metabolites synthesized by terpene synthases are the major components of essential oils and are of great economic value. In plants, synthesis of terpenes is compartmentalized into monoterpenes and diterpenes (as well as carotenoids and chlorophylls) that are produced via the 1-deoxy-d-xylulose-5-phosphate (DXP) pathway in the plastids while the sesquiterpenes and triterpenes are made in the cytosol via the mevalonate pathway[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Kirby J, Keasling JD: Biosynthesis of plant isoprenoids: perspectives for microbial engineering. Annual review of plant biology. 2009, 60: 335-355. 10.1146/annurev.arplant.043008.091955." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2238">60</a>]. The enzymes involved in the synthesis of azadirachtin have not been studied in detail. The pathway hierarchy in neem-derived tetranortriterpenoid azadirachtin-A primarily requires the synthesis of a terpenoid backbone followed by steroid and triterpenoid biosynthesis. We found that the genes in the terpenoids and steroid biosynthesis family (TPS21, lytB, ispH, ispE, GGPS, +neomenthol dehydrogenase, FDPS, FDFT1 and SQLE) were more abundant in neem compared to <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa, C. sinensis</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>.</p><p>This study may help close the gap between traditional knowledge and current practices in the agrochemical industry. The progressive increase in the cost of petroleum-based starting materials has led to a surge in the price of synthetic pesticides scourging the meager profit margins presented to the farmers in developing countries. This study may provide a cost-effective alternative by aiding biotechnological research efforts in enhancing disease resistance in plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>The <i>de novo</i> sequencing and analyses of the draft genome and organ-specific transcriptomes of neem plant, <i>Azadirachta indica</i> is reported<i>. A. indica</i> is the first <i>Meliaceae</i> family member to be sequenced. The neem genome bears fewer repetitive elements compared to other sequenced higher plants. It has about 20,000 genes with an average transcript length of 1.69 kbp. <i>A. indica</i>’s evolutionary closeness to <i>Citrus</i> species was verified by both molecular phylogenetic analyses and sequence similarity. Transcript expression and the exon-intron junction architecture of underlying genes involved in the terpenoid biosynthesis pathways suggested relative abundance of enzymes involved in the azadirachtin synthesis in neem. Genes involved in the terpenoid biosynthesis pathways in neem bear longer introns compared to the same genes in <i>A. thaliana, O. sativa, V. vinifera</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Sample collection, identification, nucleic acids extraction and quality control</h3><p>Different parts of <i>Azadirachta indica</i> A. Juss were collected from a locally grown tree. The genus and species was confirmed at the South Regional Centre, Botanical Survey of India, Tamil Nadu Agricultural University Campus, Coimbatore, Tamil Nadu, India using the herbarium of the twigs bearing flowers and fruits. All parts of the plant (root, leaf, stem, and flower) were collected and immediately flash frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at -80°C until further use. Total genomic DNA was extracted using Qiagen plant genomic DNA extraction kit. Total RNA was extracted using Plant Total RNA extraction kit (Bioteke, China). The quality, quantity of genomic DNA and integrity of total RNA were accessed using Nanodrop, Qubit methods and Agilent Bioanalyzer RNA 6000 Nano chip respectively (Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>) before using the DNA and RNA in making sequencing libraries.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Sequencing library preparation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Solexa sequencing-by-synthesis</h4><p>Short-insert paired-end sequencing libraries were prepared using Illumina (San Diego, California, USA) TruSeq library preparation kit with one modification. The library was not amplified post-adapter ligation to minimize amplification-related bias and the un-amplified, adapter-ligated library was directly used to generate clusters on cBOT instrument following the manufacturer’s recommendation. Long-insert mate pair sequencing libraries were prepared using multiple inserts (10 kbp, 3 kbp and 1.5 kbp) using Illumina mate pair library preparation kit. RNA-seq sequencing libraries from all the four organs were prepared using Illumina TruSeq-RNA library prep kit following the manufacturers instructions. Details of the methods including the QC of samples and sequencing libraries are provided in the Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Pyro-sequencing using IonTorrent Personal Genome Machine (PGM)</h3><p>Genomic DNA of neem was sheared to 200 bp using Covaris (Woburn, Massachusetts, USA) to make libraries using IonTorrent PGM protocol. Sequencing was performed following the manufacturer’s instructions. Details of the methods including the QC of libraries are provided in the Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Cloning of neem genomic DNA</h3><p>Genomic DNA (2-11 kbp) was cloned into pJAZZ-OC linear cloning vector using BigEasy v2.0 kit from Lucigen (Middleton, Wisconsin, USA) following manufacturer’s specifications. Following cloning, positive colonies were picked and sub-cultured. Plasmid DNA was isolated and checked for inserts by restriction digestion (Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>). Positive clones were selected and used for capillary sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Capillary sequencing</h3><p>Neem genomic DNA clones were used for capillary sequencing using BigDye Terminator kit using the manufacturer’s instructions. Positive clones were selected and used for sequencing in either forward or reverse direction using M13 primers using the Applied Biosystems 3500 instrument (Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19"><i>De novo</i> genome and transcriptome assembly</h3><p>Two sets of Illumina short insert (150 bp and 350 bp) paired-end libraries and three long insert (1.5 kbp, 3 kbp and 10 kbp) mate-pair libraries, capillary Sanger sequencing reads generated from cloned neem genomic DNA and pyro-sequencing reads generated with IonTorrent Personal Genome Machine (Table S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM1">1</a>) were used in assembling the neem genome with SOAPdenovo[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li R, Zhu H, Ruan J, Qian W, Fang X, Shi Z, Li Y, Li S, Shan G, Kristiansen K, et al: De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 2010, 20 (2): 265-272. 10.1101/gr.097261.109." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2367">16</a>]. We assembled the neem transcriptomes from all organs using the raw RNA-seq paired reads from four organs: root, leaf, stem and flower using the genome-independent transcriptome assembler Trinity[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Yassour M, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Amit I, Adiconis X, Fan L, Raychowdhury R, Zeng Q, et al: Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011, 29 (7): 644-652. 10.1038/nbt.1883." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2370">35</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Phylogenetic analyses</h3><p>The concatenated alignment of either the <i>rbcL</i> and <i>rbcS</i> genes of 24 plant species including neem or rbcL gene alone for <i>Meliaceae</i> family members were used to generate a bootstrapped phylogeny using Phylip v3.69[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Felsenstein J: PHYLIP - Phylogeny Inference Package (Version 3.2). Cladistics. 1989, 5: 164-166." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2390">22</a>]. For reporting the phylogenetic analysis results, we followed the “Minimal Information About a Phylogenetic Analysis” (MIAPA) standard[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Leebens-Mack J, Vision T, Brenner E, Bowers JE, Cannon S, Clement MJ, Cunningham CW, dePamphilis C, deSalle R, Doyle JJ, et al, et al: Taking the first steps towards a standard for reporting on phylogenies: Minimum Information About a Phylogenetic Analysis (MIAPA). Omics : a journal of integrative biology. 2006, 10 (2): 231-237. 10.1089/omi.2006.10.231." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2393">61</a>]. Detail attributes and the actual files are provided in Additional files<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM4">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM5">5</a> and<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM6">6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Repeat identification and analyses</h3><p>Repeat Modeler (<i>RepeatModeler Open-1.0</i>, 2008–2010)[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Repeat Modeller.&#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2417">24</a>] Repeat Masker (<i>RepeatMasker Open-3.0</i>, 1996–2010)[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Repeat Masker.&#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2423">25</a>], LTR_Finder[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu Z, Wang H: LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35: W265-268. 10.1093/nar/gkm286. Web Server issue" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2426">30</a>], TransposonPSI[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="TransposonPSI.&#xA;                    http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2430">31</a>] and MITE-Hunter[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Han Y, Wessler SR: MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 2010, 38 (22): e199-10.1093/nar/gkq862." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2433">32</a>] were used to detect, identify and characterize repeats in the neem genome. Vmatch[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Vmatch.&#xA;                    http://www.vmatch.de&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2436">33</a>] was used to cluster repeat sequences and build a consensus (&gt;80% similar) neem repeat library.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Calculation of LTR insertion age</h3><p>5' and 3' LTR sequences of each LTR-retrotransposon, identified by LTR finder[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu Z, Wang H: LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35: W265-268. 10.1093/nar/gkm286. Web Server issue" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2447">30</a>], were aligned using ClustalW MPI[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Thompson JD, Gibson TJ, Higgins DG, et al: Multiple sequence alignment using ClustalW and ClustalX. Current protocols in bioinformatics. Edited by: Baxevanis AD. 2002, Chapter 2:Unit 2 3" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2450">62</a>]. The distances between 5' and 3' LTR sequences for each alignment were calculated using the Kimura 2-parameter distmat tool from EMBOSS package[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Distmat.&#xA;                    helixweb.nih.gov/emboss/html/distmat.html&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ," href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2453">63</a>]. The insertion ages were further calculated from these distance values according to the formula[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Paterson AH, Bowers JE, Bruggmann R, Dubchak I, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hellsten U, Mitros T, Poliakov A, et al, et al: The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature. 2009, 457 (7229): 551-556. 10.1038/nature07723." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2456">28</a>] T = K / (2r), where T is the insertion age in years, K = Kimura distance value and r is the substitution rate per site per year (taken to be 1.3 × 10<sup>-8</sup> as found in <i>O. sativa</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ma J, Bennetzen JL: Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2004, 101 (34): 12404-12410. 10.1073/pnas.0403715101." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2465">64</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Nucleotide composition and expression analyses</h3><p>The nucleotide composition of the transcripts from the neem organs and genome scaffolds/chromosomes of the neem, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i> were plotted as frequency histograms and density curves. The fragments per transcript kilobase per million fragments (FPKM) provided by Trinity were log<sub>2</sub> transformed and plotted as frequency histograms for each organ, before and after categorizing the transcripts based on their G + C composition.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Transcript annotation, gene prediction and inter-species comparison analyses</h3><p>The transcripts were serially annotated for each organ using similarity-based BLAST analyses (Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>) and functionally classified using BLAST2GO analyses. Gene predictions were performed using GlimmerHMM[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL: TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004, 20 (16): 2878-2879. 10.1093/bioinformatics/bth315." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2499">49</a>] and PASA[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Haas BJ, Delcher AL, Mount SM, Wortman JR, Smith RK, Hannick LI, Maiti R, Ronning CM, Rusch DB, Town CD, et al, et al: Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (19): 5654-5666. 10.1093/nar/gkg770." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2502">38</a>]. Inter-species gene comparison was performed as described in Text S1 in Additional file<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#MOESM3">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Organ-specific transcript expression pathway analyses</h3><p>The transcripts for individual organs in neem were mapped to KEGG[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S: KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2000, 28 (1): 27-30. 10.1093/nar/28.1.27." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2516">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S, Furumichi M, Tanabe M, Hirakawa M: KEGG for representation and analysis of molecular networks involving diseases and drugs. Nucleic Acids Res. 2010, 38 (Database issue): D355-360." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2519">65</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S, Sato Y, Furumichi M, Tanabe M: KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40 (1): D109-114. 10.1093/nar/gkr988." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-13-464#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d54360e2522">67</a>] pathways using the KAAS server. The KO-assigned transcripts were divided into low and high expression categories. A heat-map of these across the four organs was computed using R. Analyses using KEGG were also carried out for other species, namely <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i>, focusing on the enzymes mapping to the azadirachtin-A biosynthesis pathway. The differential transcript expression level indices of these enzymes in neem relative to other species were compared. The gene structures for these enzymes, and the structures of the top and bottom ten transcripts ranked according to their expression levels were compared across all species.</p><p>KEGG annotations obtained for transcripts from all organs were parsed to retain only the un-annotated ones. PASA assemblies, establishing correspondence between each organ's transcripts and common genomic loci were obtained for the same. The top 300 highly expressed un-annotated transcripts for each organ were chosen, filtered for redundancies, to yield a total of 888 unique assemblies. A heat-map was plotted using the log<sub>2</sub> (FPKM) values of these transcripts using R.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Accession number</h3><p>Genome and transcriptome sequence data described in this report are submitted to the NCBI Short Read Archive database [SRA 053330].</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec27" data-title="Authors contribution"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec27-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec27">Authors contribution</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec27-content"><p>BP conceived, planned, designed and directed the project; NMK and SP designed the analytical workflow and guided the process; NMK, SP, PJ, PG, RC, and SV analyzed the data, DSA, AKH and BK produced the Solexa Illumina sequencing data. LV, JN produced the capillary sequencing data; NV, KD and KR produced and helped in the generation of pyro-sequencing data, BK and JN performed the neem genomic DNA cloning experiment; NMK, SP and BP wrote the manuscript. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p></div></div></section>
